</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:War" title="Template:War"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:War" title="Template talk:War"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:War&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1634" data-file-width="1089" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg/220px-Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg/330px-Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg/440px-Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_statue_of_dancing_warrior_480_bC_lower_italy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">Infantry</a> were the first military forces in history. This warrior statuette demonstrates that military culture was an important part of historical societies, c.480 BC, <a href="/wiki/Staatliche_Antikensammlungen" title="Staatliche Antikensammlungen">Staatliche Antikensammlungen</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Military history</b> is the study of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">armed conflict</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">history of humanity</a>, and its impact on the societies, cultures and economies thereof, as well as the resulting changes to <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">local</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relationships</a>.
</p><p>Professional historians normally focus on military affairs that had a major impact on the societies involved as well as the aftermath of conflicts, while amateur historians and hobbyists often take a larger interest in the details of battles, equipment and uniforms in use.
</p><p>The essential subjects of military history study are the causes of war, the social and cultural foundations, <a href="/wiki/Doctrine#Military_usage" title="Doctrine">military doctrine on each side</a>, the logistics, leadership, technology, <a href="/wiki/Strategy" title="Strategy">strategy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Military_tactics" title="Military tactics">tactics</a> used, and how these changed over time. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">just war theory</a> explores the moral dimensions of warfare, and to better limit the destructive reality caused by war, seeks to establish a doctrine of military ethics.
</p><p>As an applied field, military history has been studied at academies and service schools because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_and_control_(military)" title="Command and control (military)">military command</a> seeks to not repeat past mistakes, and improve upon its current performance by instilling an ability in commanders to perceive historical parallels during a battle, so as to capitalize on the lessons learned from the past.  When certifying military history instructors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combat_Studies_Institute" title="Combat Studies Institute">Combat Studies Institute</a> deemphasizes rote detail memorization and focuses on themes and context in relation to current and future conflict, using the motto "Past is Prologue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discipline of military history is dynamic, changing with development as much of the subject area as the societies and organisations that make use of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The dynamic nature of the discipline of military history is largely related to the rapidity of change the military forces, and the art and science of managing them, as well as the frenetic pace of <a href="/wiki/Technological_evolution" title="Technological evolution">technological development</a> that had taken place during the period known as the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, and more recently in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_age" title="Information age">information ages</a>.  An important recent concept is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolution_in_Military_Affairs" title="Revolution in Military Affairs">Revolution in Military Affairs</a> (RMA) which attempts to explain how warfare has been shaped by emerging technologies, such as gunpowder. It highlights the short outbursts of rapid change followed by periods of relative stability.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_versus_academic_military_history">Popular versus academic military history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Popular versus academic military history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In terms of the history profession in major countries, military history is an orphan, despite its enormous popularity with the general public. <a href="/wiki/William_H._McNeill_(historian)" title="William H. McNeill (historian)">William H. McNeill</a> points out:
</p>
<dl><dd>This branch of our discipline flourishes in an intellectual ghetto. The 144 books in question [published in 1968-78] fall into two distinct classes: works aimed at a popular readership, written by journalists and men of letters outside academic circles, and professional work nearly always produced within the military establishment.... The study of military history in universities remains seriously underdeveloped. Indeed, lack of interest in and disdain for military history probably constitute one of the strangest prejudices of the profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>In recent decades University level courses in military history remain popular; often they use films to humanize the combat experience. For example, Eugene P. A. Scleh, history professor at the University of Maine, has explored the advantages and problems of teaching a course of "Modern War and Its Images" entirely through films. Students said they found the documentaries more valuable than the dramas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>  However, military historians are frustrated by their marginal status in major history departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography_of_military_history">Historiography of military history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historiography of military history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">Historiography</a> is the study of the history and method of the discipline of history or the study of a specialised topic. In this case, military history with an eye to gaining an accurate assessment of conflicts using all available sources. For this reason military history is periodised, creating overlaying boundaries of study and analysis in which descriptions of battles by leaders may be unreliable due to the inclination to minimize mention of failure and exaggerate success. Military historians use Historiographical analysis in an effort to allow an unbiased, contemporary view of records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>One military historian, Jeremy Black, outlined problems 21st-century military historians face as an inheritance of their predecessors: Eurocentricity, a technological bias, a focus on leading military powers and dominant military systems, the separation of land from sea and recently air conflicts, the focus on state-to-state conflict, a lack of focus on political "tasking" in how forces are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p.ix,_Black_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-p.ix,_Black-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>If these challenges were not sufficient for the military historians, the limits of method are complicated by the lack of records, either destroyed or never recorded for its value as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_secret" title="Military secret">military secret</a> that may prevent some salient facts from being reported at all; scholars still do not know the exact nature of <a href="/wiki/Greek_fire" title="Greek fire">Greek fire</a> for instance. Researching Operation Enduring Freedom and Operation Iraqi Freedom, for example, have presented unique challenges to historians due to records that were destroyed to protect classified military information, among other reasons. Historians utilize their knowledge of government regulation and military organization, and employing a targeted and systematic research strategy to piece together war histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Despite these limits, wars are some of the most studied and detailed periods of human history.
</p><p>Military historians have often <a href="/wiki/Comparative_history" title="Comparative history">compared</a> organization, tactical and strategic ideas, leadership, and national support of the militaries of different nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1980s, historian <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Kimball_(historian)" title="Jeffrey Kimball (historian)">Jeffrey Kimball</a> studied the influence of a historian's political position on current events on interpretive disagreement regarding the causes of 20th century wars.  He surveyed the ideological preferences of 109 active diplomatic historians in the United States as well as 54 active military historians. He finds that their current political views are moderately correlated with their historiographical interpretations. A clear position on the left-right continuum regarding capitalism was apparent in most cases.  All groups agreed with the proposition, "historically, Americans have tended to view questions of their national security in terms of such extremes as good vs. evil."  Though the Socialists were split, the other groups agreed that "miscalculation and/or misunderstanding of the situation" had caused U.S. interventionism."  Kimball reports that:
</p>
<dl><dd>Of historians in the field of diplomatic history, 7% are Socialist, 19% are Other, 53% are Liberal, 11% are None and 10% Conservative. Of military historians, 0% are Socialist, 8% are Other, 35% are Liberal, 18% are None and 40% are Conservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_resources">Online resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Online resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>People interested in military history from all periods of time, and all subtopics, are increasingly turning to the Internet for many more resources than are typically available in nearby libraries.  Since 1993, one of the most popular sites, with over 4000 members (subscriptions are free) has been H-WAR, sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/H-Net" title="H-Net">H-Net</a> network based at Michigan State University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> H-War has six coeditors, and an academic advisory board that sets policy.  It sponsors daily moderated discussions of current topics, announcements of new publications and conferences, and reports on developments at conferences. The H-Net family of lists has sponsored and published over 46,000 scholarly book reviews, thousands of which deal with books in military history broadly conceived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>  Wikipedia itself has a very wide coverage of military history, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Military_history/Assessment#statistics" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Military history/Assessment">with over 180,000 articles</a>. Its editors sponsor <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Military_history" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Military history">Wikipedia:WikiProject Military history</a> and encourage readers to join.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_war_museums">Military and war museums<span class="anchor" id="military_museum"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Military and war museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg/220px-Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg/330px-Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg/440px-Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canadianwarmuseummgs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_War_Museum" title="Canadian War Museum">Canadian War Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Military_and_war_museums" title="Category:Military and war museums">Category:Military and war museums</a></div>
<p>Military museums specialize in military histories; they are often organized from a national point of view, where a museum in a particular country will have displays organized around conflicts in which that country has taken part. They typically take a broad view of warfare's role in the nation's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>  They typically include displays of weapons and other military equipment, <a href="/wiki/Military_uniform" title="Military uniform">uniforms</a>, wartime <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, and exhibits on civilian life during wartime, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_orders,_awards_and_decorations" title="Military orders, awards and decorations">decorations</a>, among others. A military museum may be dedicated to a particular or area, such as the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_War_Museum_Duxford" title="Imperial War Museum Duxford">Imperial War Museum Duxford</a> for military aircraft, <a href="/wiki/German_Tank_Museum" title="German Tank Museum">Deutsches Panzermuseum</a> for tanks, the <a href="/wiki/Lange_Max_Museum" title="Lange Max Museum">Lange Max Museum</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front (World War I)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Spy_Museum" title="International Spy Museum">International Spy Museum</a> for espionage, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberty_Memorial#The_National_World_War_I_Museum" title="Liberty Memorial">The National World War I Museum</a> for <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the "D-Day Paratroopers Historical Center" (Normandy) for WWII airborne, or more generalist, such as the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_War_Museum" title="Canadian War Museum">Canadian War Museum</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Arm%C3%A9e" title="Musée de l'Armée">Musée de l'Armée</a>. For the Italian alpine wall one can find the most popular museum of bunkers in the small museum <a class="external text" href="https://www.n8bunker.com/" rel="nofollow">n8bunker</a> at Olang / Kronplatz in the heard of the dolomites of <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">South Tyrol</a>.  The U.S. Army and the state National Guards operate 98 military history museums across the United States and three abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Curators debate how or whether the goal of providing diverse representations of war, in terms of positive and negative aspects of warfare.  War is seldom presented as a good thing, but soldiers are heavily praised. David Lowenthal has observed that in today's museums, "nothing seems too horrendous to commemorate". Yet as Andrew Whitmarsh notes, "museums frequently portray a sanitised version of warfare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The actual bomber that dropped the atomic bomb on Japan became the focus of an angry national controversy with veterans attacking curators and historians when the Smithsonian Institution planned to put its fuselage on public display in 1995. The <a href="/wiki/Enola_Gay#Exhibition_controversy" title="Enola Gay">uproar</a> led to cancellation of the exhibit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_historians">Early historians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early historians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The documentation of military history begins with the confrontation between Sumer (current <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>) and Elam (current <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) c. 2700 BC near the modern <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>. Other prominent records in military history are the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a> in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> (though its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historicity_of_the_Iliad" title="Historicity of the Iliad">historicity</a> has been challenged), <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (484 BC – 425 BC) who is often called the "father of history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Next was <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> whose impartiality, despite being an <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenian</a>, allowed him to take advantage of his exile to research the war from different perspectives by carefully examining documents and interviewing eyewitnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> An approach centered on the analysis of a leader was taken by <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> (430 BC – 355 BC) in <i><a href="/wiki/Anabasis_(Xenophon)" title="Anabasis (Xenophon)">Anabasis</a></i>, recording the expedition of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Younger" title="Cyrus the Younger">Cyrus the Younger</a> into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.
</p><p>The records of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> (100 BC – 44 BC) enable a comparative approach for <a href="/wiki/Military_campaign" title="Military campaign">campaigns</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Civili" title="Commentarii de Bello Civili">Commentarii de Bello Civili</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_evolution">Technological evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Technological evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_military_technology" title="History of military technology">History of military technology</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>The nature of warfare <i>never</i> changes, only its superficial manifestations. <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">Joshua</a> and <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hector" title="Hector">Hector</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a> would recognize the combat that our soldiers and Marines have waged in the alleys of Somalia and Iraq. The uniforms evolve, bronze gives way to titanium, arrows may be replaced by laser-guided bombs, but the heart of the matter is still killing your enemies until any survivors surrender and do your will.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Peters" title="Ralph Peters">Ralph Peters</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh,_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2514" data-file-width="1899" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg/200px-Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg/300px-Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg/400px-Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh%2C_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rams%C3%A9s_II_en_Qadesh,_relieve_de_Abu_Simbel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramses_II" title="Ramses II">Ramses II</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a> fighting at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">Battle of Kadesh</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>New weapons development can dramatically alter the face of war, the cost of warfare, the preparations, and the training of soldiers and leaders. A rule of thumb is that if your enemy has a potentially war winning weapon, you have to either match it or neutralize it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_era">Ancient era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ancient era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">Chariots</a> originated around 2000 BC. The chariot was an effective, fast weapon; while one man controlled the maneuvering of the chariot, a second bowman could shoot arrows at enemy soldiers. These became crucial to the maintenance of several governments, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Egyptian_Kingdom" title="New Egyptian Kingdom">New Egyptian Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> and the nation states of the early to middle <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the military unit types and technologies which were developed in the ancient world are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sling_(weapon)" title="Sling (weapon)">Slinger</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">Hoplite</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliaries_(Roman_military)" title="Auxiliaries (Roman military)">Auxiliaries</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">Infantry</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">Archery</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chariots" title="Chariots">Chariots</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">Cavalry</a></li></ul>
<p>For settled agrarian civilizations, the infantry became the core of military action. The infantry started as opposing armed groups of soldiers underneath commanders. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> and early Romans used rigid, heavily armed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" title="Phalanx formation">phalanxes</a>. The Macedonians and Hellenistic states would adopt phalanx formations with <a href="/wiki/Sarissa" title="Sarissa">sarissa</a> pikemen. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Army" title="Roman Army">Romans</a> would later adopt more flexible <a href="/wiki/Maniple_(military_unit)" title="Maniple (military unit)">maniples</a> from their neighbors which made them extremely successful in the field of battle. The kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a> in East Asia also adopted infantry combat, a transition from chariot warfare from centuries earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">Archers</a> were a major component of many ancient armies, notably those of the Persians, Scythians, Egyptians, Nubians, Indians, Koreans, Chinese, and Japanese.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">Cavalry</a> became an important tool. In the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Expedition" title="Sicilian Expedition">Sicilian Expedition</a>, led by Athens in an attempt to subdue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Italy" title="Syracuse, Italy">Syracuse</a>, the well-trained Syracusan cavalry became crucial to the success of the Syracusans. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> effectively deployed his cavalry forces to secure victories. In battles such as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Battle of Cannae</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Persian_Wars" title="Roman-Persian Wars">Roman-Persian Wars</a>, the importance of the cavalry would be repeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archer" title="Horse archer">horse archers</a>, who had the ability to shoot on horseback – the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, and other various steppe people were especially fearsome with this tactic. By the 3rd–4th century AD, <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavily armored cavalry</a> became widely adopted by the Parthians, <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Byzantines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_dynasty" title="Eastern Han dynasty">Eastern Han dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a>, etc.
</p><p>The early <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranians</a> developed the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_chariot" title="War chariot">chariots</a> in warfare. The <a href="/wiki/Scythed_chariot" title="Scythed chariot">scythed chariot</a> was later invented in India and soon adopted by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">War elephants</a> were sometimes deployed for fighting in ancient warfare. They were first used in India and later adopted by the Persians. War elephants were also used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes_River" title="Battle of the Hydaspes River">Battle of the Hydaspes River</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> against the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> One of the most important military transactions of the ancient world was <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a>'s gift of 500 elephants to <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trireme.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="522" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trireme.jpg/250px-Trireme.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trireme.jpg/375px-Trireme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trireme.jpg/500px-Trireme.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trireme.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Greek <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">trireme</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">Naval warfare</a> was often crucial to military success. Early navies used sailing ships without cannons; often the goal was to ram the enemy ships and cause them to sink. There was human oar power, often using slaves, built up to ramming speed. <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">Galleys</a> were used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3000_BC" title="3000 BC">3rd millennium BC</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Cretans</a>. The Greeks later advanced these ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1210 BC, the first recorded naval battle was fought between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suppiluliuma_II" title="Suppiluliuma II">Suppiluliuma II</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, which was defeated. In the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>, the navy became of increasing importance.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">Triremes</a> were involved in more complicated sea-land operations. <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a> helped to build up a stronger Greek navy, composed of 310 ships, and defeated the Persians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>, ending the Persian invasion of Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>, the war between <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and Rome started with an advantage to Carthage because of their naval experience. A Roman fleet was built in 261 BC, with the addition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corvus_(weapon)" title="Corvus (weapon)">corvus</a> that allowed Roman soldiers to board enemy ships. The bridge would prove effective at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mylae" title="Battle of Mylae">Battle of Mylae</a>, resulting in a Roman victory.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, in the 8th century AD, invented a ship propelled by oars with a dragon decorating the prow, hence called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drekar" title="Drekar">Drakkar</a>. The 12th century AD <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a> invented ships with watertight bulkhead compartments while the 2nd century BC <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> invented rudders and sculled oars for their warships.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">Fortifications</a> are important in warfare. Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hill-fort" title="Hill-fort">hill-forts</a> were used to protect inhabitants in the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. They were primitive forts surrounded by ditches filled with water.  Forts were then built out of mud bricks, stones, wood, and other available materials. Romans used rectangular fortresses built out of wood and stone. As long as there have been fortifications, there have been contraptions to break in, dating back to the times of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> and earlier. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_warfare" title="Siege warfare">Siege warfare</a> is often necessary to capture forts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle-ages">Middle-ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Middle-ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1044" data-file-width="4262" decoding="async" height="59" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg/240px-Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg/360px-Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg/480px-Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Takezaki_suenaga_ekotoba3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> boarding <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> ships in 1281</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the military unit types and technologies which were used in the medieval period are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">Artillery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">Cataphract</a></li>
