<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(weapon)" title="Bow (weapon)">Bows</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrow_(weapon)" title="Arrow (weapon)">arrows</a> were often used by combatants. Egyptians shot arrows from chariots effectively. The <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> was developed around 500 BC in China, and was used heavily in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The English/Welsh <a href="/wiki/Longbow" title="Longbow">longbow</a> from the 12th century also became important in the Middle Ages. It helped to give the English a large early advantage in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>, even though the English were eventually defeated. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Crécy">Battle of Crécy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a> are excellent examples of how to destroy an enemy using a longbow. It dominated battlefields for over a century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder">Gunpowder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gunpowder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="638" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/200px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/300px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/400px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of an "eruptor," a proto-cannon, capable of firing cast-iron bombs filled with gunpowder, from the 14th century Ming Dynasty book Huolongjing</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cannon_pic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="853" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cannon_pic.jpg/260px-Cannon_pic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cannon_pic.jpg/390px-Cannon_pic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cannon_pic.jpg/520px-Cannon_pic.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cannon_pic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A small <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>-era cannon</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:155fire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/155fire.jpg/220px-155fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/155fire.jpg/330px-155fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/155fire.jpg/440px-155fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:155fire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 155 mm <a href="/wiki/M198_howitzer" title="M198 howitzer">M198 howitzer</a> firing a shell</div></div></div>
<p>There is evidence for <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> evolving slowly from formulations by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_alchemy" title="Chinese alchemy">Chinese alchemists</a> as early as the 4th century, at first as experiments for life force and metal transmutation, and later experiments as pyrotechnics and incendiaries. By the 10th century, the developments in gunpowder led to many new weapons that were improved over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The Chinese used incendiary devices based on this in siege warfare against the Mongols starting in the mid 13th century. "Pots with wicks of flax or cotton were used, containing a combination of sulfur, saltpeter (<a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Aconitine" title="Aconitine">aconitine</a>, oil, resin, ground charcoal and wax."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> argued the Chinese were able to destroy buildings and walls using such devices. Such experimentation was not present in Western Europe, where the combination of saltpeter, sulfur and charcoal were used exclusively for explosives and as a propellant in firearms. What the Chinese often referred to as the "fire drug" arrived in Europe, fully fleshed out, as gunpowder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">Cannons</a> were first used in Europe in the early 14th century, and played a vital role in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>. The first cannons were simply welded metal bars in the form of a cylinder, and the first cannonballs were made of stone. By 1346, at the Battle of Crécy, the cannon had been used; at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a> they would be used again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first infantry firearms, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_lances" title="Fire lances">fire lances</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannons</a>, were held in one hand, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explosive_charge" title="Explosive charge">explosive charge</a> was ignited by a lit match or hot coal held in the other hand. In the mid-15th century came the <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a>, allowing the gun to be aimed and fired while held steady with both hands, as used in the <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">arquebus</a>. Starting about 1500, clever but complicated firing mechanisms were invented to generate sparks to ignite the powder instead of a lit match, starting with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheel_lock" title="Wheel lock">wheel lock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snaplock" title="Snaplock">snaplock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snaphance" title="Snaphance">snaphance</a>, and finally the <a href="/wiki/Flintlock_mechanism" title="Flintlock mechanism">flintlock mechanism</a>, which was simple and reliable, becoming standard with the <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">musket</a> by the early 17th century.
</p><p>At the beginning of the 16th century, the first European <a href="/wiki/Fire_ship" title="Fire ship">fire ships</a> were used. Ships were filled with flammable materials, set on fire, and sent to enemy lines. This tactic was successfully used by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> to scatter the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> at the Battle of <a href="/wiki/Gravelines" title="Gravelines">Gravelines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and would later be used by the Chinese, Russians, Greeks, and several other countries in naval battles.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_mines" title="Naval mines">Naval mines</a> were invented in the 17th century, though they were not used in great numbers until the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. They were used heavily in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First</a> and Second World Wars. Air-deployed naval mines were used to mine the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Vietnamese" title="North Vietnamese">North Vietnamese</a> port of <a href="/wiki/Haiphong" title="Haiphong">Haiphong</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Navy" title="Iraqi Navy">Iraqi Navy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> used naval mines extensively during the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War#Attacks_on_shipping" title="Iran–Iraq War">Tanker War</a>, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>.
</p><p>The first navigable <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> was built in 1624 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cornelius_Drebbel" title="Cornelius Drebbel">Cornelius Drebbel</a>, it could cruise at a depth of 15 feet (5 m). However, the first military submarine was constructed in 1885 by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Peral" title="Isaac Peral">Isaac Peral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Turtle" title="American Turtle"><i>Turtle</i></a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/David_Bushnell" title="David Bushnell">David Bushnell</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fulton" title="Robert Fulton">Robert Fulton</a> then improved the submarine design by creating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautilus_(submarine)" title="Nautilus (submarine)"><i>Nautilus</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Howitzer" title="Howitzer">Howitzer</a>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery" title="Field artillery">field artillery</a>, was developed in the 17th century to fire high trajectory explosive shells at targets that could not be reached by flat trajectory projectiles.
</p><p>Organizational changes resulting in better training and intercommunication, made the concept <a href="/wiki/Combined_arms" title="Combined arms">combined arms</a> possible, allowing the use of infantry, cavalry, and <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> in a coordinated way.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Bayonets also became of wide usage to infantry soldiers. <a href="/wiki/Bayonet" title="Bayonet">Bayonet</a> is named after <a href="/wiki/Bayonne" title="Bayonne">Bayonne</a>, France where it was first manufactured in the 16th century. It is used often in infantry charges to fight in hand-to-hand combat. General <a href="/wiki/Jean_Martinet" title="Jean Martinet">Jean Martinet</a> introduced the bayonet to the French army. They were used heavily in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and continued to be used in modern wars like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" title="Invasion of Iraq">Invasion of Iraq</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balloon_(aircraft)" title="Balloon (aircraft)">Balloons</a> were first used in warfare at the end of the 18th century. It was first introduced in Paris of 1783; the first balloon traveled over 5 miles (8 km). Previously military <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">scouts</a> could only see from high points on the ground, or from the mast of a ship. Now they could be high in the sky, signalling to troops on the ground. This made it much more difficult for troop movements to go unobserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of the 18th century, iron-cased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artillery_rocket" title="Artillery rocket">artillery rockets</a> were successfully used militarily in India against the British by <a href="/wiki/Tipu_Sultan" title="Tipu Sultan">Tipu Sultan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mysore" title="Kingdom of Mysore">Kingdom of Mysore</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Mysore_Wars" title="Anglo-Mysore Wars">Anglo-Mysore Wars</a>. Rockets were generally inaccurate at that time, though <a href="/wiki/William_Hale_(British_inventor)" title="William Hale (British inventor)">William Hale</a>, in 1844, was able to develop a better rocket. The new rocket no longer needed the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rocket_stick&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rocket stick (page does not exist)">rocket stick</a>, and had a higher accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1860s there were a series of advancements in <a href="/wiki/Rifle" title="Rifle">rifles</a>. The first <a href="/wiki/Repeating_rifle" title="Repeating rifle">repeating rifle</a> was designed in 1860 by a company bought out by <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Repeating_Arms_Company" title="Winchester Repeating Arms Company">Winchester</a>, which made new and improved versions. <a href="/wiki/Springfield_rifle" title="Springfield rifle">Springfield rifles</a> arrived in the mid-19th century also. <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">Machine guns</a> arrived in the late 19th century. <a href="/wiki/Automatic_rifle" title="Automatic rifle">Automatic rifles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_machine_gun" title="Light machine gun">light machine guns</a> first arrived at the beginning of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the later part of the 19th century, the self-propelled <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a> was developed. The <a href="/wiki/HNoMS_Rap_(1873)" title="HNoMS Rap (1873)">HNoMS Rap</a> was the world's first torpedo boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_guns_and_artillery">Early guns and artillery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early guns and artillery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_firearm" title="History of the firearm">History of the firearm</a></div>
<p>The fire lance, the predecessor of the gun, was invented in China between the tenth and eleventh century.  The barrel was originally designed out of bamboo shoots, later with metal. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham_Professor_of_Chinese_History,_Science,_and_Civilization" title="Joseph Needham Professor of Chinese History, Science, and Civilization">Joseph Needham</a> notes "all the long preparations and tentative experiments were made in China, and everything came to Islam and the West fully fledged, whether it was the fire lance or the explosive bomb, the rocket or the metal-barrel handgun and bombard."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup> By the 1320s Europe had guns, but scholars state that the exact time and method of migration from China remains a mystery. Evidence of firearms is found in Iran and Central Asia in the late fourteenth century.  It was not until roughly 1442 that guns were referenced in India.  Reliable references to guns in Russia begins around 1382.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>An illustration of a "pot-shaped gun" found in the Holkham Hall Milemete manuscript dated to 1326 shows earliest advent of firearms in European history. The illustration shows an arrow, set in the pot-shaped gun pointed directly at a structure. Archaeological evidence of such "gun arrows" were discovered in <a href="/wiki/Eltz_Castle" title="Eltz Castle">Eltz Castle</a>, "dated by relation to a historical event (a feud with the Archbishop of Trier in 1331-36 leading to a siege), seem to confirm again that this was at least one of the types of guns like the Milemete used in these very early examples."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Peter Fraser Purton, the best evidence of the earliest gun in Europe is the Loshult gun, dated to the fourteenth century. Discovered in 1861, the Loshult was made of bronze measured 11.8 inches in length. A replica of the Loshult was created, using similar gunpowder compounds with present-day materials, to determine the effectiveness of the weapon. The Gunpowder Research Group, who designed the recreation, found that at high elevations, the Loshult could fire as far as 1300 meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-51">[51]</a></sup>  Though inaccurate, missing targets further than 200 meters, the Loshult could fire a range of projectiles such as arrows and shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup> It was determined that the Loshult could be effectively fired at ranks of soldiers and structures.
</p><p>Written works from the Cabinet des Titres of the Imperial Library of Paris has found evidence of canons in France in 1338.  The works illustrate canons being used on-board ships at the Rouen during that time.  "...an iron Fire-arm, which was provided with forty-eight bolts, made of iron and freather; also one pound of saltpetre and half a pound of sulphur to make the powder propel arrows."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have been unable to determine the sizes of these cannons and others, outside the artifacts recovered.  Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Brackenbury" title="Henry Brackenbury">Henry Brackenbury</a> was able to surmise the approximate size of these cannons by comparing receipts for both the firearms and the corresponding amounts of gunpowder purchased.  The receipts show a transaction for "25 Livres for 5 canons."  Brackenbury was able to deduce, when comparing the costs of the cannons and the gunpowder apportioned, that they each iron cannon weighed approximately 25 lbs, while the brass cannons weighed roughly 22 lbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_the_Bold" title="Philip the Bold">Philip the Bold</a> (1363-1404) is credited<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> with creating the most effective artillery power in Europe in the late fourteenth century, effectively creating the Burgundian estate.  Philip's development of a large artillery army made the small country a reputable force against larger empires such as England and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>  Philip had achieved this by establishing a large scale artillery manufacturing economy in Burgundy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>  Philip used his new cache of artillery to help the French capture an English-held fortress of Odruik.  The artillery used to take Odruik used cannonballs measuring to about 450 pounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Large artillery was a major contributing factor to the fall of Constantinople at the hands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a> (1432-1481).  Having resigned his position as ruler due to youth and inexperience in 1446, Mehmed moved to the Ottoman capital of Manisa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>  After his uncle, <a href="/wiki/Murad_II" title="Murad II">Murad II</a> died in 1451, Mehmed once again became Sultan.  He turned his attention to claiming the Byzantine capital, Constantinople.  Mehmed, like Philip, started mass-producing cannons by enticing craftsmen to his cause with money and freedom.  For 55 days, Constantinople was bombarded with artillery fire, throwing cannonballs as large as 800 lbs at its walls.  On May 29, 1453, Constantinople fell into Ottoman control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_firearm_tactics">Early firearm tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early firearm tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As guns and artillery became more advanced and prevalent, so to did the tactics by which they were implemented.  According to Historian Michael Roberts "...a military revolution began with the broad adoption of firearms and artillery by late sixteenth-century European armies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stavros_2013_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stavros_2013-55">[55]</a></sup>  Infantry with firearms replaced cavalry.  Empires adapted their strongholds to withstand artillery fire.  Eventually drilling strategies and battlefield tactics were adapted for the evolution in firearms use.
</p><p>In Japan, at the same time during the sixteenth-century, this military evolution was also taking hold.  These changes included a universal adoption of firearms, tactical developments for effective use, logistical restructuring within the military itself, and "the emergence of centralized and political and institutional relationships indicative of the early modern order."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stavros_2013_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stavros_2013-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tactically, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">Oda Nobunaga</a>, the technique known as "volleying" or countermarch drills were implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-6"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>  Volley fire is an organized implementation of firearms, where infantry are structured in ranks.  The ranks will alternate between loading and firing positions, allowing more consistent rates of fire and preventing enemies from taking over a position while members reload.
</p><p>Historical evidence shows that Oda Nobunaga implemented his volley technique successfully in 1575, twenty years before evidence of such a technique is shown in Europe.  The first indications of the countermarch technique in Europe was by Lord William Louis of Nassau (1538-1574) in the mid-1590s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stavros_2013_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stavros_2013-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-7"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Korea also seemed to be adapting the volley technique, earlier than even the Japanese.  "Koreans seem to have employed some kind of volley principle with guns by 1447, when the Korean King Sejong the Great instructed his gunners to shoot their 'fire barrels' in squads of five, taking turns firing and loading."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-8"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>This was on display during what Kenneth Swope called the First Great East Asian War, when Japan was trying to take control and subjugate Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a> (1537–1598) made a failed invasion of Korea, which lasted six years, eventually pushed back by the Koreans with the aid of Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_40-9"><a href="#cite_note-:14-40">[40]</a></sup>  Japan, using overwhelming firepower, had many early victories on the Korean peninsula's.  Though the Korean's had similar manpower, "the curtain of arrows thrown up by defenders was wiped out by (Japanese) gunfire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stavros_2013_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stavros_2013-55">[55]</a></sup> After the Japanese were finally pushed back in 1598, sweeping military reforms took place in Korea, largely based on updating and implementing the volley technique with firearms.
</p><p>It was <a href="/wiki/Qi_Jiguang" title="Qi Jiguang">Qi Jiguang</a>, a Ming Chinese General that provided the original treatise, disseminated to Koreans, that aided in this venture.  In these manuals, Qi "...gave detailed instructions in the use of small group tactics, psychological warfare, and other 'modern' techniques."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-56">[56]</a></sup>  Qi emphasized repetitive drilling, dividing men into smaller groups, separating the strong from weak.  Qi's ethos was one of synthesizing smaller groups, trained in various tactical formations, into larger companies, battalions and armies.  By doing this they could "operate as eyes, hands, and feet..." aiding to overall unit cohesion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-:02-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_technologies">Modern technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Modern technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the start of the World Wars, various nations had developed weapons that were a surprise to their adversaries, leading to a need to learn from this, and alter how to combat them. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flame_thrower" title="Flame thrower">Flame throwers</a> were first used in the First World War. The French were the first to introduce the <a href="/wiki/Armored_car_(military)" title="Armored car (military)">armored car</a> in 1902. Then in 1918, the British produced the first <a href="/wiki/Armoured_personnel_carrier" title="Armoured personnel carrier">armored troop carrier</a>. Many early <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tanks</a> were proof of concept but impractical until further development. In World War I, the British and French held a crucial advantage due to their superiority in tanks; the Germans had only a few dozen A<sub>7</sub>V tanks, as well as 170 captured tanks. The British and French both had several hundred each. The French tanks included the 13 ton <a href="/wiki/Schneider_CA1" title="Schneider CA1">Schneider CA1</a>, with a 75 mm gun, and the British had the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_I_tank#Mark_IV" title="Mark I tank">Mark IV</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_I_tank#Mark_V_series" title="Mark I tank">Mark V</a> tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 17, 1903, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wright_Brothers" title="Wright Brothers">Wright Brothers</a> performed the first controlled, powered, heavier-than-air flight; it went 39 meters (120 ft). In 1907, the first <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">helicopter</a> flew, but it wasn't practical for usage. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_War_I_Aviation" title="World War I Aviation">Aviation</a> became important in World War I, in which several <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">aces</a> gained fame. In 1911 an aircraft took off from a warship for the first time. Landings on a cruiser were another matter. This led to the development of an <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> with a decent unobstructed flight deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">Chemical warfare</a> exploded into the public consciousness in World War I but may have been used in earlier wars without as much human attention. The Germans used gas-filled shells at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bolimov" title="Battle of Bolimov">Battle of Bolimov</a> on January 3, 1915. These were not lethal, however. In April 1915, the Germans developed a chlorine gas that was highly lethal, and used it to moderate effect at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Ypres" title="Second Battle of Ypres">Second Battle of Ypres</a>. Gas masks were invented in matter of weeks, and poison gas proved ineffective at winning battles. It was made illegal by all nations in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>World War II gave rise to even more technology. The worth of the aircraft carrier was proved in the battles between the United States and Japan like the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">Battle of Midway</a>. <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">Radar</a> was independently invented by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" title="Axis Powers">Axis powers</a>. It used <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> to detect objects. <a href="/wiki/Molotov_cocktail" title="Molotov cocktail">Molotov cocktails</a> were invented by General Franco in the Spanish Civil War, directing the Nationalists to use them against Soviet tanks in the assault on Toledo. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> was developed by the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">dropped on Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> in 1945, quickly ending World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, the main powers engaged in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">Nuclear arms race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_race" title="Space race">space race</a>, both nations attempted to launch human beings into space to the moon. Other technological advances centered on intelligence (like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_satellite" title="Spy satellite">spy satellite</a>) and missiles (<a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic missiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a>). <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_submarine" title="Nuclear submarine">Nuclear submarine</a>, invented in 1955. This meant submarines no longer had to surface as often, and could run more quietly. They evolved into becoming underwater missile platforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods_of_military_history">Periods of military history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Periods of military history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_warfare">Ancient warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ancient warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_warfare" title="Ancient warfare">Ancient warfare</a></div>
<p>Much of what we know of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a> is the history of militaries: their conquests, their movements, and their technological innovations. There are many reasons for this. Kingdoms and empires, the central units of control in the ancient world, could only be maintained through military force. Due to limited agricultural ability, there were relatively few areas that could support large communities, so fighting was common.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">Weapons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armor" title="Armor">armor</a>, designed to be sturdy, tended to last longer than other artifacts, and thus a great deal of surviving artifacts recovered tend to fall in this category as they are more likely to survive. Weapons and armor were also mass-produced to a scale that makes them quite plentiful throughout history, and thus more likely to be found in archaeological digs.
</p><p>Such items were also considered signs of prosperity or virtue, and thus were likely to be placed in tombs and monuments to prominent warriors. And writing, when it existed, was often used for kings to boast of military conquests or victories.
</p><p>Writing, when used by the common man, also tended to record such events, as major battles and conquests constituted major events that many would have considered worthy of recording either in an epic such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric" title="Homeric">Homeric</a> writings pertaining to the Trojan War, or even personal writings. Indeed, the earliest stories center on warfare, as war was both a common and dramatic aspect of life; the witnessing of a major battle involving many thousands of soldiers would be quite a spectacle, even today, and thus considered worthy both of being recorded in song and art, but also in realistic histories, as well as being a central element in a fictional work.
</p><p>Lastly, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation_states" title="Nation states">nation states</a> evolved and empires grew, the increased need for order and efficiency lead to an increase in the number of records and writings. Officials and armies would have good reason for keeping detailed records and accounts involving any and all things concerning a matter such as warfare that, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Sun_Tzu" title="Sun Tzu">Sun Tzu</a>, was "a matter of vital importance to the state". For all these reasons, military history comprises a large part of ancient history.
</p><p>Notable militaries in the ancient world included the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persians</a>, Ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> (notably the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonians</a>), <a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Nubia" title="Military of ancient Nubia">Kushites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_India" title="Military history of India">Indians</a> (notably the <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadhas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandharas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cholas" title="Cholas">Cholas</a>), Early Imperial <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">Chinese</a> (notably the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_(state)" title="Han (state)">Han</a> Dynasties), <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> Confederation, Ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertile_crescent" title="Fertile crescent">fertile crescent</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> was the center of several prehistoric conquests. Mesopotamia was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a> and Persians. <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranians</a> were the first nation to introduce <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> into their army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suren-Pahlav_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suren-Pahlav-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> began growing as an ancient power, but eventually fell to the <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libyans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubians</a>, Assyrians, Persians, Greeks, Romans, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arabs</a>.
</p><p>The earliest recorded battle in <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Ten_Kings" title="Battle of the Ten Kings">Battle of the Ten Kings</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Indian_epic_poetry" title="Indian epic poetry">Indian epics</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i> are centered on conflicts and refer to military formations, theories of warfare and esoteric weaponry. <a href="/wiki/Chanakya" title="Chanakya">Chanakya</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a></i> contains a detailed study on ancient warfare, including topics on <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> invaded Northwestern India and defeated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Porus" title="King Porus">King Porus</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes_River" title="Battle of the Hydaspes River">Battle of the Hydaspes River</a>. The same region was soon re conquered by <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> after defeating the Macedonians and <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucids</a>. He also went on to conquer the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanda_Dynasty" title="Nanda Dynasty">Nanda Empire</a> and unify Northern India. Most of Southern Asia was unified under his grandson <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka the Great</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_War" title="Kalinga War">Kalinga War</a>, though the empire collapsed not long after his reign.
</p><p>In China, the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> had risen and collapsed. This led to a <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, in which several states continued to fight with each other over territory. Philosopher-strategists such as <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sun_Tzu" title="Sun Tzu">Sun Tzu</a> wrote various manuscripts on ancient warfare (as well as international diplomacy).
</p><p>The Warring States era philosopher <a href="/wiki/Mozi" title="Mozi">Mozi</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micius" title="Micius">Micius</a>) and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohist" title="Mohist">Mohist</a> followers invented various siege weapons and siegecraft, including the Cloud Ladder (a four-wheeled, extendable ramp) to scale fortified walls during a siege of an enemy city. The warring states were first unified by <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> after a series of military conquests, creating the first empire in China.
</p><p>His <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_Dynasty" title="Qin Dynasty">empire</a> was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, which expanded into Central <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, Northern China/Manchuria, Southern China, and present day Korea and Vietnam. The Han came into conflict with settled people such as the <a href="/wiki/Wiman_Joseon" title="Wiman Joseon">Wiman Joseon</a>, and proto-Vietnamese Nanyue. They also came into conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>), <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, and other steppe civilizations.
</p><p>The Han defeated and drove the Xiongnus west, securing the city-states along the silk route that continued into the <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthian</a> Empire. After the decline of central imperial authority, the Han Dynasty collapsed into an era of civil war and continuous warfare during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period in the 3rd century AD.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> Persian Empire was founded by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> after conquering the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. His successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambyses_II_of_Persia" title="Cambyses II of Persia">Cambyses</a> went on to conquer the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Empire" title="Egyptian Empire">Egyptian Empire</a>, much of Central Asia, and parts of Greece, India and Libya. The empire later fell to Alexander the Great after defeating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III</a>. After being ruled by the Seleucid dynasty, the Persian Empire was subsequently ruled by the Parthian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid</a> dynasties, which were the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>'s greatest rivals during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Persian_Wars" title="Roman-Persian Wars">Roman-Persian Wars</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, several <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> rose to power, including <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>. The Greeks successfully stopped two Persian invasions, the first at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a>, where the Persians were led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius the Great</a>, and the second at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>, a naval battle where the Greek ships were deployed by orders of <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a> and the Persians were under <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, and the land engagement of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> then erupted between the two Greek powers Athens and Sparta. Athens built a long wall to protect its inhabitants, but the wall helped to facilitate the spread of a plague that killed about 30,000 Athenians, including <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>. After a disastrous campaign against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Italy" title="Syracuse, Italy">Syracuse</a>, the Athenian navy was decisively defeated by <a href="/wiki/Lysander" title="Lysander">Lysander</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aegospotami" title="Battle of Aegospotami">Battle of Aegospotami</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonians" title="Ancient Macedonians">Macedonians</a>, underneath <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> and Alexander the Great, invaded Persia and won several major victories, establishing Macedonia as a major power. However, following Alexander's death at an early age, the empire quickly fell apart.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="2760" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/250px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/375px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/500px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 3rd-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Ludovisi_sarcophagus" title="Great Ludovisi sarcophagus">Great Ludovisi sarcophagus</a> depicts a battle between <a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military of ancient Rome">Romans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, Rome was gaining power, following a rebellion against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscans</a>. During the three <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, the Romans defeated the neighboring power of Carthage. The <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a> centered on <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">naval warfare</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> started with <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a>'s invasion of Italy by crossing the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>. He famously won the encirclement at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Battle of Cannae</a>. However, after <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio</a> invaded Carthage, Hannibal was forced to follow and was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">Battle of Zama</a>, ending the role of Carthage as a power.
</p><p>After defeating Carthage the Romans went on to become the Mediterranean's dominant power, successfully campaigning in Greece, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aemilius_Paulus" title="Aemilius Paulus">Aemilius Paulus</a> decisive victory over Macedonia at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pydna" title="Battle of Pydna">Battle of Pydna</a>), in the Middle East (<a href="/wiki/Lucullus" title="Lucullus">Lucius Licinius Lucullus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Magnus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus">Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus</a>), in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> (<a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Gaius Julius Caesar</a>) and defeating several <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a>). While Roman armies suffered several major losses, their large population and ability (and will) to replace battlefield casualties, their training, organization, tactical and technical superiority enabled Rome to stay a predominant military force for several centuries, utilizing well trained and maneuverable armies to routinely overcome the much larger "tribal" armies of their foes (see Battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aquae_Sextiae" title="Battle of Aquae Sextiae">Aquae Sextiae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vercellae" title="Battle of Vercellae">Vercellae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tigranocerta" title="Battle of Tigranocerta">Tigranocerta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alesia" title="Battle of Alesia">Alesia</a>).
</p><p>In 54 BC the Roman triumvir <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a> took the offensive against the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> in the east. In a decisive battle at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Carrhae</a> Romans were defeated and the golden <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(Roman)" title="Aquila (Roman)">Aquilae</a> (legionary battle standards) were taken as trophies to <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. The battle was one of the worst defeats suffered by the Roman Republic in its entire history.
</p><p>While successfully dealing with foreign opponents, Rome experienced numerous civil wars, notably the power struggles of Roman generals such as Marius and <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a> during the end of the Republic. Caesar was also notable for his role in the civil war against the other member of the Triumvirate (Pompey) and against the Roman Senate.
</p><p>The successors of Caesar – Octavian and Mark Anthony, also fought a civil war with Caesar's assassins (Senators Brutus, Cassius, etc.). Octavian and Mark Anthony eventually fought another civil war between themselves to determine the sole ruler of Rome. Octavian emerged victorious and Rome was turned into an empire with a huge standing army of professional soldiers.
</p><p>By the time of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, the Romans had expanded to the Atlantic Ocean in the west and to <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> in the east and controlled Northern Africa and Central Europe up to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. However, Aurelius marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty#Five_Good_Emperors" title="Nerva–Antonine dynasty">Five Good Emperors</a>, and Rome quickly fell into decline.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hun" title="Hun">Huns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>, and other barbaric groups invaded Rome, which continued to suffer from <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> and other internal strifes. Despite the attempts of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_(emperor)" title="Constantine I (emperor)">Constantine I</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>, western Rome collapsed and was eventually conquered in 476. The <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantine empire</a> continued to prosper, however.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_warfare">Medieval warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medieval warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_warfare" title="Medieval warfare">Medieval warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1755" data-file-width="1998" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/220px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/330px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg/440px-Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_crecy_froissart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Crécy">Battle of Crécy</a> (1346) between the English and French in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stirrups" title="Stirrups">stirrups</a> came into use some time during the <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a> militaries were forever changed. This invention coupled with technological, cultural, and social developments had forced a dramatic transformation in the character of warfare from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>, changing military tactics and the role of <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a>.
</p><p>Similar patterns of warfare existed in other parts of the world. In China around the 5th century armies moved from massed infantry to cavalry based forces, copying the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomads</a>. The Middle East and North Africa used similar, if often more advanced, technologies than Europe.
</p><p>In Japan the Medieval warfare period is considered by many to have stretched into the 19th century. In Africa along the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">Sudan</a> states like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sennar" title="Kingdom of Sennar">Kingdom of Sennar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulani_Empire" title="Fulani Empire">Fulani Empire</a> employed Medieval tactics and weapons well after they had been supplanted in Europe.
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">Medieval</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> was firmly implanted, and there existed many landlords in Europe. Landlords often owned <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a> to protect their territory.
</p><p>The Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Arab Empire</a> began rapidly expanding throughout the Middle East, North Africa, and Central Asia, initially led by <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, and later under the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyads</a>. While their attempts to invade Europe by way of the Balkans were <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">defeated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> the Arabs expanded to the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> in the west and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus Valley</a> in the east. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abassid" title="Abassid">Abassids</a> then took over the Arab Empire, though the Umayyads remained in control of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a>.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Tours</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> stopped short a Muslim invasion. The Abassids defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang</a> Chinese army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a>, but were later defeated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turk" title="Seljuk Turk">Seljuk Turks</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongols</a> centuries later, until the Arab Empire eventually came to an end after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Battle of Baghdad (1258)">Battle of Baghdad</a> in 1258.
</p><p>In China, the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> had risen and conquered the Chen Dynasty of the south. They invaded Vietnam (northern Vietnam had been in Chinese control since the Han dynasty), fighting the troops of <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>, who had cavalry mounted on elephants. After decades of economic turmoil and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goguryeo-Sui_Wars" title="Goguryeo-Sui Wars">failed invasion of Korea</a>, the Sui collapsed and was followed by the Tang dynasty, who fought with various <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> groups, the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_people" title="Tibetan people">Tibetans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lhasa" title="Lhasa">Lhasa</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanguts" title="Tanguts">Tanguts</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a>, and collapsed due to political fragmentation of powerful regional military governors (jiedushi). The innovative <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> followed next, inventing new weapons of war that employed the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Fire" title="Greek Fire">Greek Fire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> (see section below) against enemies such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jurchens" title="Jurchens">Jurchens</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Autor_nieznany_(malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego),_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4469" data-file-width="7131" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg/220px-Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg/330px-Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg/440px-Autor_nieznany_%28malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego%29%2C_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Autor_nieznany_(malarz_z_kr%C4%99gu_Lukasa_Cranacha_Starszego),_Bitwa_pod_Orsz%C4%85.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The victory of the <a href="/wiki/Muscovite%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Wars" title="Muscovite–Lithuanian Wars">Polish-Lithuanian</a> forces over the Muscovites at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Orsha" title="Battle of Orsha">Battle of Orsha</a> in 1514</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongols</a> under <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> conquered most of Eurasia. They took over China, Persia, Turkestan, and Russia. After Kublai Khan took power and created the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, the divisions of the empire ceased to cooperate with each other, and the Mongol Empire was only nominally united.
</p><p>In New Zealand, prior to European discovery, oral histories, legends and <a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a> include many stories of battles and wars. <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> warriors were held in high esteem. One group of <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a> migrated to the <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Islands" title="Chatham Islands">Chatham Islands</a>, where they developed the largely pacifist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moriori_people" title="Moriori people">Moriori</a> culture. Their pacifism left the Moriori unable to defend themselves when the islands were invaded by mainland Māori in the 1830s.
</p><p>They proceeded to massacre the Moriori and enslave the survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warrior" title="Warrior">Warrior</a> culture also developed in the isolated <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>. During the 1780s and 1790s the chiefs and alii were constantly fighting for power. After a series of battles the Hawaiian Islands were united for the first time under a single ruler who would become known as <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_I" title="Kamehameha I">Kamehameha I</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder_warfare">Gunpowder warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gunpowder warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gunpowder_warfare" title="Gunpowder warfare">Gunpowder warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sebastiaen_Vrancx_(1573-1647)_-_Veldslag_(1640)_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2244" data-file-width="3312" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg/220px-Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg/330px-Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg/440px-Sebastiaen_Vrancx_%281573-1647%29_-_Veldslag_%281640%29_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sebastiaen_Vrancx_(1573-1647)_-_Veldslag_(1640)_-_Sevilla_Bellas_Artes_22-03-2011_12-07-38.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eighty_Years%27_War" title="Eighty Years' War">Eighty Years' War</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Dutch Revolt</a> against Spain, painting by <a href="/wiki/Sebastiaen_Vrancx" title="Sebastiaen Vrancx">Sebastiaen Vrancx</a></div></div></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> weapons were first developed in <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> China (see also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_of_Song_Dynasty" title="Technology of Song Dynasty">Technology of Song Dynasty</a>), the technology later spread west to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, from where it spread to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" title="Safavid Empire">Safavid Empire</a> of Persia and the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> of India. The <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">arquebus</a> was later adopted by European armies during the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Wars" title="Italian Wars">Italian Wars</a> of the early 16th century.
</p><p>This all brought an end to the dominance of armored cavalry on the battlefield. The simultaneous decline of the feudal system – and the absorption of the medieval city-states into larger states – allowed the creation of professional standing armies to replace the <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal levies</a> and mercenaries that had been the standard military component of the Middle Ages.
</p><p>In Africa, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Ibrihim_al-Ghazi" title="Ahmad ibn Ibrihim al-Ghazi">Ahmad ibn Ibrihim al-Ghazi</a>, was the first African commander to use gunpowder on the continent in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian%E2%80%93Adal_War" title="Ethiopian–Adal War">Ethiopian–Adal War</a>, that lasted for fourteen years (1529–1543).
</p><p>The period spanning between the 1648 <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> and the 1789 <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> is also known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabinettskriege" title="Kabinettskriege">Kabinettskriege</a></i> (Princes' warfare) as wars were mainly carried out by imperial or monarchics states, decided by cabinets and limited in scope and in their aims. They also involved quickly shifting alliances, and mainly used mercenaries.
</p><p>Over the course of the 18th-19th centuries all military arms and services underwent significant developments that included a more mobile <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery" title="Field artillery">field artillery</a>, the transition from use of battalion <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Infantry_drill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Infantry drill (page does not exist)">infantry drill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Close_order_formation" title="Close order formation">close order</a> to open order formations and the transfer of emphasis from the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayonets" title="Bayonets">bayonets</a> to the rifle that replaced the musket, and virtual replacement of all types of cavalry with the universal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragoons" title="Dragoons">dragoons</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_Revolution">Military Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Military Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_Revolution" title="Military Revolution">Military Revolution</a></div>
<p>The Military Revolution is a conceptual schema for explaining the transformation of European military strategy, tactics and technology in the early modern period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The argument is that dramatic advances in technology, government finance, and public administration transformed and modernized European armies, tactics, and logistics. Since warfare was so central to the European state, the transformation had a major impact on modernizing government bureaucracies, taxation, and the national economy. The concept was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Roberts_(historian)" title="Michael Roberts (historian)">Michael Roberts</a> in the 1950s as he focused on <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Sweden 1560–1660</a>. Roberts emphasized the introduction of muskets that could not be aimed at small targets, but could be very effective when fired in volleys by three ranks of infantry soldiers, with one firing while the other two ranks reloaded.  All three ranks march forward to demolish the enemy. The infantry now had the firepower that had been reserved to the artillery, and had mobility that could rapidly advance in the battlefield, which the artillery lacked. The infantry thereby surpassed the artillery in tactical maneuvering on the battlefield.  Roberts linked these advances with larger historical consequences, arguing that innovations in tactics, drill and doctrine by the Dutch and Swedes 1560–1660 led to a need for more and better trained troops and thus for permanent forces (standing armies).  Armies grew much larger and more expensive. These changes in turn had major political consequences in the level of administrative support and the supply of money, men and provisions, producing new financial demands and the creation of new governmental institutions. "Thus, argued Roberts, the modern art of war made possible—and necessary—the creation of the modern state".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In the 1990s the concept was modified and extended by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Parker_(historian)" title="Geoffrey Parker (historian)">Geoffrey Parker</a>, who argued that developments in fortification and siege warfare caused the revolution.  The concept of a military revolution based upon technology has given way to models based more on a slow evolution in which technology plays a minor role to organization, command and control, logistics and in general non-material improvements. The revolutionary nature of these changes was only visible after a long evolution that handed Europe a predominant place in warfare, a place that the industrial revolution would confirm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of a military revolution in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries has received a mixed reception among historians. Noted military historians Michael Duffy and <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Black_(historian)" title="Jeremy Black (historian)">Jeremy Black</a> have strongly criticised it as misleading, exaggerated and simplistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_warfare">Industrial warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Industrial warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_warfare" title="Industrial warfare">Industrial warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bataille_Lizaine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="845" data-file-width="1274" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bataille_Lizaine.jpg/220px-Bataille_Lizaine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bataille_Lizaine.jpg/330px-Bataille_Lizaine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bataille_Lizaine.jpg/440px-Bataille_Lizaine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bataille_Lizaine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a></div></div></div>
<p>As weapons—particularly small arms—became easier to use, countries began to abandon a complete reliance on professional soldiers in favor of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a>. Technological advances became increasingly important; while the armies of the previous period had usually had similar weapons, the industrial age saw encounters such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sadowa" title="Battle of Sadowa">Battle of Sadowa</a>, in which possession of a more advanced technology played a decisive role in the outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>  Conscription was employed in industrial warfare to increase the number of military personnel that were available for combat. Conscription was notably used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> and the major parties during the two World Wars.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">Total war</a> was used in industrial warfare, the objective being to prevent the opposing nation to engage in war.  Napoleon was the innovator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">William Tecumseh Sherman</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Sherman%27s_March_to_the_Sea" title="Sherman's March to the Sea">March to the Sea</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a>'s burning of the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_Valley" title="Shenandoah Valley">Shenandoah Valley</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> were examples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On the largest scale the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II" title="Strategic bombing during World War II">strategic bombing of enemy cities and industrial factories</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> was total warfare.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_warfare">Modern warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Military_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Modern warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Modern_warfare" title="Modern warfare">Modern warfare</a></div>
<p>Since the 1940s, preparation for a major war has been based on technological arms races involving all sorts of new weapons systems, such as nuclear and biological, as well as computerized control systems, and the opening of new venues, such as seen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_race" title="Space race">Space race</a> involving the United States, the Soviet Union, and more recently, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern war also saw the improvement of <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">armored tank</a> technology. While tanks were present in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>, armored warfare technology came to a head with the start of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Many of the technologies commonly seen on main battle tanks today, such as <a href="/wiki/Composite_armour" title="Composite armour">composite armor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tank_gun" title="Tank gun">high caliber cannons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tank#Active_protection_system" title="Tank">advanced targeting systems</a>, would be developed during this time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A distinctive feature since 1945 is the absence of wars between major powers—indeed the near absence of any traditional wars between established countries. The major exceptions were the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1971" title="Indo-Pakistani War of 1971">Indo-Pakistani War of 1971</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a> 1980–1988, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> of 1990–91.  Instead actual fighting has largely been a matter of civil wars and insurgencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The most recent example of a war between two nation states would be the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine</a>.
</p>
